[10/23/25, 7:01:16â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢ In Defense of Doctors â€¢

Most people live their young adult lives free of chronic diseases (Iâ€™m not referring to infectious diseases here). When you visit a doctor for the first time as an adultâ€”usually around the age of 45â€“50â€”and he checks your blood pressure, blood sugar, cholesterol, and other markers, he has no idea about the trajectory of those markers.

For example, if your blood test shows an A1C of 7.8, he doesnâ€™t know how rapidly that number has been increasing. Is it rising at a rate of 0.1 per month, or just 0.01? In that situation, it is prudent for him to assume the worst-case scenario. So, he prescribes medication. His objectives are twofold:

1. To change the trajectory and prevent the disease from getting worse.
2. To bring the marker into a range recommended by the professional association that governs his specialty.

For instance, if the doctor is a general practitioner and a member of the American College of Physicians (ACP), the guideline is to keep A1C below 8.0. His goal is simply to prevent the progression of diabetes, so a small dose of medication might be sufficient.

If the doctor is a member of the American Diabetes Association (ADA), he aims to bring the A1C below 7.0 and will likely prescribe a higher dose.

If the doctor is an endocrinologist and a member of the American Association of Clinical Endocrinologists (AACE), his target is an A1C below 6.5. He may prescribe two medications.

Most people, after leaving the doctorâ€™s office and filling the prescription, start taking their medications regularlyâ€”but only make minor changes to their diet and lifestyle. The most common ones include replacing sugar with artificial sweeteners and switching from regular soft drinks to diet sodas (which, as we will see in later posts, can actually worsen the condition).

Now, if a particular patientâ€”like yourselfâ€”is more conscientious and makes significant changes to diet and lifestyle, the numbers may improve dramatically by the time of the next doctorâ€™s visit. However, when you ask the doctor to stop the medication, he may strongly resist. Thatâ€™s because, during his medical training, he was taught that chronic diseases cannot be cured. He firmly believes your improved markers are due solely to the medication.

In my experience working with over 250,000 members, I encounter this situation frequently. Sooner or later, each one of you will face this same dilemma. Thatâ€™s why I am raising this issue nowâ€”before we begin our in-depth discussion of chronic diseases starting next week. Each of you will need to find your own path and, eventually, have this conversation with your doctor.
[10/23/25, 7:01:17â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢Alternate Medicine:Â 
Is it Only a Placebo Effect?â€¢

Many who share success stories with alternative medicine arenâ€™t lying or trying to promote an agenda. Some of these stories seem miraculous, turning people into lifelong believers. However, not all such successes are due to the placebo effect. My goal is to explain how various factors can influence our judgment and contribute to the spread of myths. Here are some factors to consider:

1. The human body is a self-healing organism, constantly striving to repair itself. Cells die and regenerate regularly, with cycles ranging from days to months.

2. When weâ€™re sick, we tend to improve our diet and lifestyle unconsciously, which can aid recovery.

3. Because of these factors, the body usually heals itself. Recovery times vary based on the illness, ranging from weeks to years.

4. In times of illness, we often start by consulting traditional medical professionals. If treatments donâ€™t work, we may turn to alternative therapies like Homeopathy, Ayurveda, or Acupuncture.

5. Sometimes, recovery coincides with trying alternative treatments, leading us to mistakenly attribute healing to these therapies. In reality, the body may have healed on its own.

6. These perceived successes are then shared as proof of alternative medicineâ€™s effectiveness, even though the treatment may not have been responsible.

7. Success stories get more attention than failures, which are rarely published. This creates a bias, as we are more likely to hear about positive outcomes, further spreading the idea that alternative therapies work when conventional medicine doesnâ€™t.

If youâ€™re reading this, youâ€™re likely a health-conscious individual. Keep these points in mind when you hear about miraculous recoveries. For more insight, check out the documentaries *What The Health* and *Death by Medicine*.
[10/24/25, 7:01:06â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢SIDE EFFECTS and ADRS, OF DRUGSâ€¢

Most of us are aware of the concept of drug side effects, which can be either negative or positive. The goal of any drug manufacturer is to minimize negative side effects. If side effects are severe, a drug is unlikely to receive approval from regulatory authorities. Additionally, most side effects are evaluated over short time frames; therefore, effects that only become apparent after prolonged useâ€”such as over decadesâ€”may go undetected. Drug manufacturers often aim to minimize the disclosure of side effects, revealing only those they cannot conceal or are legally required to disclose.

Itâ€™s important to recognize that all individuals are epigenetically different, so just as specific foods affect us in varied ways, specific drugs also have different effects on each person. This variability makes it difficult to identify all side effects during drug trials. It is not unreasonable to assume that there are certain side effects that your doctor may not be aware of. For example, it has long been known that many diabetic medications can increase the risk of heart disease. However, manufacturers of these medications are only required to demonstrate that their drugs lower blood glucose levels, not that they do not increase the risk of heart disease.Â 

I personally had to stop using Avandia after discovering its potential risks, and I switched to Actos, only to stop that as well for the same reason. This led me to change my diet and lifestyle.

If you are a diabetic, your doctor may be so concerned about your risk of heart disease that they recommend you take statins as a preventive measure. Interestingly, the reverse is also true: if you are a heart patient taking certain drugs, your likelihood of developing diabetes significantly increases.

Understanding Side Effects and Adverse Drug Reactions (ADRs)

The distinction between side effects and adverse drug reactions (ADRs) is often misunderstood. A side effect refers to unintended effects of a drug that occur within the therapeutic range, usually an undesirable secondary effect in addition to the desired therapeutic outcome. Side effects can vary among individuals.

An adverse drug reaction (ADR) is defined as â€œa response to a medicinal product that is noxious and unintended.â€ It is estimated that between 3% and 7% of all hospitalizations are due to ADRs, which can occur at normal therapeutic doses. In contrast, drug overdoses represent a different issue entirely.

The leading cause of ADRs is multiple drug interactions. It is crucial for individuals taking several medications to check for potential adverse interactions. The situation becomes even more complex as individuals can respond differently to each drug. The Food and Drug Administration (FDA) in the U.S. maintains a database of ADRs, which healthcare providers are required to report and is publicly accessible. Medscape also offers a drug interaction checker that can be useful. Of course, discontinuing medications altogether would eliminate this concern.
[10/24/25, 7:01:07â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢NNT & NNH OF DRUGSâ€¢

Having explained the concepts of side effects and ADRs, letâ€™s discuss how various drugs are evaluated in terms of their performance. Two critical criteria in drug evaluation are Number Needed to Treat (NNT) and Number Needed to Heal (NNH).

Number Needed to Treat (NNT)Â refers to the number of patients a doctor or researcher needs to treat to prevent one additional adverse outcome, such as death. For instance, if a cancer drug reduces the mortality rate from 30% to 25%, the NNT is calculated as 1 divided by the reduction in mortality rate (0.30 - 0.25), resulting in an NNT of 20.

Number Needed to Harm (NNH)Â refers to the number of patients who need to be treated for one to experience a harmful side effect. For many drugs, the NNH is often lower than the NNT. For example, for statin drugs, the NNH for muscle pain is 21, meaning one in every 21 patients may experience muscle pain.

A tutorial video linked below will help clarify the concepts of NNT and NNH, as well as illustrate how drug companies may legally misrepresent the benefits of their products through statistical jargon.

To illustrate, consider statin drugs. According to The NNT Group, the NNT for statins prescribed for five years to prevent heart disease in individuals without prior heart issues is 104. This means 104 people must take statins for five years to prevent one additional heart attack. Meanwhile, the NNH of 21 indicates that five out of those 104 patients will likely experience muscle loss.

It is alarming to note that when all causes of death from ADRs, adverse drug events (ADEs), medical procedures, and diagnostics are considered, medical care itself becomes a leading cause of death in America, surpassing heart disease and cancer.

The Statistics for Statin drugs is shown below as a verbatim quotation:

â€œIn Summary, for those who received statins:

Benefits in NNT
No statistically significant mortality benefit
1 in 217 avoided a nonfatal heart attack (myocardial infarction)
1 in 313 avoided a nonfatal stroke

Harms in NNH
1 in 21 experienced pain from muscle damage
1 in 204 developed diabetes mellitusâ€

[10/24/25, 7:01:14â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢BIAS IN MEDICAL & NUTRITIONAL RESEARCHâ€¢

We often assume that conclusions about foods and medications from research studies are honest and accurate. Historically, most nutrition research was government-funded, with key organizations like the NIH in the U.S. and NIN in India. However, budget cuts in the 21st century have led to private industries, particularly food and Pharma, filling the funding gap. Medical and nutritional research can be categorized as follows:

(1) Primary ResearchÂ 
Traditionally funded by the NIH, it focuses on broad public benefits like laboratory studies and randomized trials. Projects like the Human Genome and Human Proteome are still NIH-funded. However, a decrease in government funding has made this research less common.

(2) Applied Research
Applied research uses science to prevent and treat diseases. While some still receive NIH funding, much is now backed by pharma, food, and the supplement industries. It can be divided into research supporting various foods, justifying supplements, or showing harm in certain foods or lifestyles.

(3) Public Health Research
This aims to improve public health at a population level, ideally government-funded, though private industries are increasingly stepping in.

(4) Clinical research
Focused on testing medications, this research is primarily funded by pharmaceutical companies. Variability in how people respond to treatments (e.g., due to gut microbiomes) complicates results. For instance, if a drug helps 30% of participants in a placebo group and 70% in an experimental group, itâ€™s deemed effective, leading to its prescription for all, even though 60% who either were not cured or cured only due to placebo effect, are still exposed to side effects without gaining any benefit.Â 

(5) Pharmacological Research
This field explores how chemicals interact with biological systems, playing a central role in pharmaceutical development. Some key points worth noting are:

5.1 The goal is not necessarily to cure diseases, as doing so could conflict with the long-term profitability of pharmaceutical companies.

5.2 Pharmaceutical companies often focus on managing health markers such as blood pressure, blood sugar, or cholesterol, which usually require increasing dosages over time.

5.3 These companies emphasize early detection, claiming it saves lives. While this is true for certain conditions like cancer, for most lifestyle diseases, it often leads to starting medication earlier. Additionally, they lobby to lower the thresholds for starting treatment, as seen with medications for blood pressure, blood sugar, and cholesterol.

5.4 Pharmaceutical companies promote tighter control of these markers, often suggesting higher or more frequent dosages. For example, increasing the dosage from two to three pills a day represents a 50% increase in business. This approach influenced the ACCORD study (2001-2009). However, the study backfired, as it showed increased mortality rates in the experimental group, leading to its early termination.

Bias in Research
When we read research papers, we expect them to be honest and objective. However, various factors can distort this objectivity. Numerous studies have shown how research can be manipulated to favor the organization funding it. Some of these biases are summarized below.Â 

(1) Funding Bias
In the U.S., government funding for research has significantly declined over the past five decades. By 2021, private sources contributed $633 billion to research and development (R&D), while government funding amounted to just $76 billion. Private funding often comes with specific interests or conditions, and studies show that research funded by private sources rarely contradicts the interests of the sponsor. This can happen for several reasons:

- Research may be designed to avoid negative outcomes. For example, using the Standard American Diet (SAD) as a control can make almost any alternative diet seem healthier by comparison.

- In some cases, research design can obscure significant negative outcomes. For instance, one study asked college students to consume large amounts of white bread, and their health appeared to improve. However, concluding that white bread is healthy would be misleading, as the students had to reduce their intake of more harmful foods like eggs, sausages, and bacon.Â 

-Funders may cancel research midway if they realize the results will not favor them. To prevent this, a law was enacted requiring that research be registered with government authorities before it begins, ensuring it cannot be canceled midstream. However, this law is not always strictly enforced by bureaucrats who are hoping for a cozy consulting career upon retirement.Â 

- Before any research is published, a synopsis must be provided. Funders often manipulate this summary to downplay negative findings. A notable example is when Senator Bob Dole altered the language of George McGovern's 1977 dietary guidelines. Originally recommending reduced meat consumption, the guidelines were changed to encourage the consumption of lean meat. The two statements have entirely different implications.

(2) Myth Bias
Myths can skew researchers' judgment. For example, high protein intake was long recommended, despite evidence that excessive protein harms kidneys and liver. Similarly, the myth that plant protein is incomplete has been debunked, yet it persisted for years.Â 
The dairy industry perpetuated the idea that milk is the best calcium source, even when evidence suggested it could contribute to osteoporosis.

(3) Scholarly Pride Bias
Respected experts may distort findings to defend earlier positions. An example is Dr. Ancel Keys, whose *Seven Countries Study* promoted low-fat diets by excluding data from 15 countries that contradicted his hypothesis.

(4) Publication Bias
Positive outcomes are more likely to be published, skewing perceptions. Negative results often face reluctance in publication, leading to a distorted understanding of certain therapies.
For example, if three studies find that egg white therapy cures second-stage breast cancer, all three studies will likely get published. However, if seven subsequent studies show it does not, only one or two may be published. Future researchers may then mistakenly conclude the therapy is effective, leading to further studies based on flawed premises.

(5) Media Bias
Media prefers sensational stories, which can lead to biased reporting. The food and pharma industries, significant advertisers, influence the coverage, leading to overexposure of certain diet trends while scientifically sound approaches are underreported. For example, Dr. Atkins' high-protein, high-fat diet received more media coverage than Pritikinâ€™s low-fat, high-carb approach, despite the latter being scientifically sounder. Sensational books like *Grain Brain* and *Wheat Belly* also received more coverage, influenced by powerful Food industry lobbies that support such narratives. Additionally, since the media relies heavily on advertising revenue from the food and pharmaceutical industries, they often hesitate to publish studies critical of these sectors.

(6) Government Bias
Government agencies often face conflicts of interest, as bureaucrats seek consulting jobs post-retirement, and elected officials raise funds from industries they regulate.Â 
The public relies on Government agencies like the Food & Drug Administration (FDA) to ensure that medical devices are safe.
There is an inherent conflict of interest within the drug and device approval process, as the FDA primarily depends on clinical trials conducted by the very companies that manufacture these products. Research findings are often ambiguous and open to debate. The FDA has, at times, approved drugs and devices despite objections from its own staff. This recently came to light on a device called MitraClip made by Abbott Labs to fix leaky heart valves that was approved by the FDA in 2013. Over 17,000 reports have been filed complaining about this device.Â 

(7) Other Conflicts of InterestÂ 
Political, personal ambitions, and other conflicts can further complicate the research landscape.

Conclusion
Itâ€™s crucial to approach research with caution. Understanding potential biases, especially the source of funding, helps reveal the full story behind the findings. Always scrutinize before accepting conclusions.

Weâ€™ve discussed how wealthy industries like Food, Pharma, and Health Care influence the information we receive. Now, letâ€™s examine the roles of Government and Media. By â€œgovernment,â€ I mean both elected politicians and bureaucrats.

Politicians need funding to win elections every 4-5 years, and that money often comes from wealthy industries, so itâ€™s natural for them to support industry interests. Lobbyists work closely with politiciansâ€™ staff to ensure this support is secured.Â 

Meanwhile, bureaucrats often seek well-paid positions post-retirement, and there are plenty of opportunities within the food and pharma industries to accommodate them. Consequently, many senior officials are willing to accommodate lobbyistsâ€™ requests. All they need is some science to justify their actions, which researchersâ€”often industry-fundedâ€”readily provide.

Letâ€™s now consider educational institutions, where much of the research is conducted. Imagine you are applying for a Ph.D. program at a leading U.S. university and need financial assistance. This assistance might come from, say, a soft drink manufacturer eager to fund research showing that consuming sugary sodas in moderation can be part of a balanced diet. The company provides funding annually. By the end of the first year, if your research findings are negative, the project might be canceled, and funding cut. This situation encourages researchers to use careful language in reports and potentially manipulate data to achieve favorable outcomes.Â 

The data isnâ€™t falsified, but choosing specific words or omitting data can still lead to misleading conclusions. Once published in a medical journal, the research paper includes an abstract. Doctors often only read the abstract, so lobbyists work hard to influence the language used here. Professor J. P. Rivers of the Department of Human Nutrition, London School of Hygiene and Tropical Medicine, observed:

	â€œThe dispassionate objectivity of scientists is a myth. No scientist is simply involved in the single-minded pursuit of truth; they are also engaged in the passionate pursuit of research grants and professional success.â€

Role of Media
Now, letâ€™s talk about the role of media and medical journals. Editors have various options for each issue and can select whichever articles they prefer.Â 

Most published media rely on advertising revenue for their income. The proceeds from magazine sales alone donâ€™t even cover the cost of publication. The largest advertisers are often the big food and pharmaceutical industries. Therefore, it is natural for publishers to avoid controversial content that might jeopardize their relationships with advertisers.

Another factor to consider is article reprints. For example, if you like an article in a magazine likeÂ TIME, you can purchase reprints for about $3â€“5 each. The cover page shows the magazine cover, but inside, only the article you requested is included.

Imagine youâ€™re an editor, deciding which health articles to include in the next issue. A dairy industry lobbyist approaches you and suggests that if you publish an article supporting the health benefits of butter, heâ€™ll order half a million reprints to distribute to doctors across the U.S. What would you choose as the editor? That order could bring in nearly a million dollars in extra revenue for the magazine, and if your compensation includes profit-sharing, it could result in a substantial bonus for you.

Itâ€™s important to understand that this scenario isnâ€™t a conspiracy. No laws are broken, and thereâ€™s no bribery involved. However, the consumer ends up misled, and situations like this happen every day. Posted below as an example is the issue ofÂ TIMEÂ magazine zone Iâ€™m referring to.ğŸ‘‡

[10/25/25, 6:51:26â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: WHO & SUGAR
In 1776, when the United States gained independence, Americans consumed about 4 pounds of sugar per person annuallyâ€”about 5 grams daily. By 1850, this had increased to 20 pounds per person, and by 1994, it reached 120 pounds. Over the past half-century, the food industry has increasingly used sugar to sweeten foods and beverages, often substituting it with high-fructose corn syrup, which is cheaper and widely available in the U.S. When efforts are made to limit sugar use, the industry often responds with resistance and pressure to halt those initiatives. Margaret Chan, Director-General of the World Health Organization, summarized this challenge:

[10/26/25, 7:03:46â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢â€¢ My Oatmeal Recipe â€¢â€¢

Oatmeal is one of the best grains for breakfast. It is rich in fiber, low in glycemic and insulin load, and keeps you full for an extended period. I recommend it to everyone, especially diabetics.

Here is my recipe for two servings:
1. 4 tbsp steel-cut oats
2. 2 tsp ground flaxseed
3. 1 tsp chia seeds
4. 1 tsp sesame seeds
5. 2 or 3 dates
6. 1 tbsp raisins
7. 1/4 cup almond milk
8. 5 walnuts
9. 1/4 tsp ground cardamom (ilaychi)
10. 1/2 tsp cinnamon
11. 1/2 cup sliced banana, blueberries, mango, or any fruit of your choice

Instructions:
Combine the first 7 ingredients with 2 cups of water and bring to a boil. Simmer for 10â€“15 minutes. Once cooked, add the cardamom, cinnamon, walnuts, and fruits.

This breakfast is a great way to meet your daily needs for nuts, seeds, and omega-3s. It tastes delicious and, when paired with a glass of green juice and a bowl of fruit, it covers two additional food groups for the day.

[10/27/25, 6:30:02â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢â€¢â€¢ EARLY DETECTION VS PREVENTION â€¢â€¢â€¢

Another area of concern is the strong emphasis in modern medicineâ€”and especially on social mediaâ€”on early detection of disease. We are constantly told to go for regular screenings to catch conditions like cancer early. Self-monitoring is also heavily promoted.

But hereâ€™s the irony: while early detection gets all the attention, prevention gets almost none. This is especially unfortunate because most lifestyle diseasesâ€”such as diabetes, heart disease, obesity, and some cancersâ€”are entirely preventable through diet and lifestyle modifications. Yet, articles and campaigns promoting such prevention are rare.

Why? Because there is no money in prevention. It threatens the profits of:
- the processed food and animal agriculture industries,
- healthcare providers who benefit from treating chronic disease,
- and the pharmaceutical industry that sells lifelong medications.

Early detection, on the surface, sounds like a good idea. But it implicitly accepts disease as inevitable. The far better approach is to prevent disease from arising in the first place. Unfortunately, if everyone started living healthier lives, many industriesâ€”processed food, animal products, hospitals, and drug companiesâ€”would take a financial hit.

To be clear, this doesnâ€™t mean thereâ€™s an organized conspiracy. But it does raise an important question: Why is prevention being ignored?

Some leaders in the medical field have spoken out. Dr. Neal Barnard, for instance, founded the organization â€œDoctors Against Irresponsible Medicineâ€, which promotes prevention through lifestyle medicine. The American Board of Internal Medicine has also launched a campaign called â€œChoosing Wiselyâ€, encouraging patients and doctors to question unnecessary treatments and focus on evidence-based practices.

Consumers need to be aware of this built-in bias. The system heavily favors early detection over prevention, not because itâ€™s better for patients, but because itâ€™s better for business.

Itâ€™s time we shift our focus from managing disease to eliminating its causes. True health comes not from pills or early tests, but from the daily choices we make around food, movement, and lifestyle.

[10/28/25, 6:39:31â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: UNNECESSARY TESTS

I previously discussed that what is â€œnormalâ€ for one group of people may not be the same for anotherâ€”especially when comparing non-vegetarians and vegans. For example, prescribing medication or supplements to a vegan simply because a blood parameter falls outside the normal range can be misguided. Itâ€™s essential to consider other factors in such cases.

Similarly, advising people to undergo numerous tests without cause is not prudent. Tests should be conducted only when there is reason to suspect something may be wrong and when a patient has reported symptoms.

This issue has been debated in the United States for some time. As a result of this debate a foundation was created by the American Board of Internal Medicine (ABIM) with the primary purpose of improving public health. The mission statement on their website is:

â€œTo advance the core values of medical professionalism as a force to improve the quality of health care.â€

Programs supporting this work include theÂ 
*Choosing WiselyÂ®ï¸ campaign,
*â€œTeaching Value and Choosing Wisely Challengeâ€,Â 
* Â â€œPutting Stewardship into Medical Education and Trainingâ€ grant program.

Of these, â€œChoosing Wiselyâ€ is of special interest to us. The goal of this campaign is toÂ promote conversations between clinicians and patients by helping patients choose care that is:
* Supported by evidence
* Not duplicative of other tests or procedures already received
* Free from harm
* Truly necessary

It calls upon leading medical specialty societies and other organizations to identify tests or procedures commonly used in their field whose necessity should be questioned and discussed with patients. Doctors are often criticized for prescribing unneeded tests and procedures that harm more than they help and add to medical costs that could otherwise be avoided.Â 

Since the campaign was launched in 2012, more than 400 tests and procedures have been called into question by 70 medical specialty societies with more than half a million member doctors. â€œMillions of Americans are increasingly realizing that when it comes to health care, more is not necessarily better,â€ said Dr. Christine Cassel, president of the ABIM Foundation. â€œThrough these lists of tests and procedures, we hope to encourage conversations between physicians and patients about what care they truly need.â€

TwelveÂ medical tests and procedures are now being questioned worldwide as unnecessary and potentially cause some harmful results to patients are listed below.Â 

12 Medical Tests to Avoid

1. Avoid Inducing Labor or having a C-Section Before 39 Weeks:
Deliveries before 39 weeks are associated with increased risks of learning disabilities, respiratory issues, and other complications. While early delivery is sometimes necessary, the recommendation is clear: a mature fetal lung test alone, without other medical reasons, is not enough to justify delivery before 39 weeks.Â 
(American College of Obstetricians and Gynecologist; American Academy of Family Physicians)Â 

2. Avoid Routine Annual Pap Tests:
For women at average risk, annual Pap tests (cervical cytology screenings) do not offer added benefits over less frequent screening. (American College of Obstetricians and Gynecologists)

3. Avoid CT Scans To Evaluate Minor Head Injuries:
About half of children who visit emergency departments for head injuries receive a CT scan, exposing them to radiation that can increase future cancer risk. These scans involve radiation levels approximately 100 times that of a standard chest X-ray. Observation should precede any decision about CT scanning. (American Academy of Pediatrics)

4. Avoid Stress Tests Using Echocardiographic Images:
For asymptomatic individuals, stress echocardiography adds limited value in assessing cardiovascular risk and may not justify its use. (American Society of Echocardiography)

5. Avoid Prescribing Type 2 Diabetes Medication To Achieve Tight Glycemic Control:
For older adults with Type 2 diabetes, there is no evidence that strict blood sugar control improves health outcomes. In fact, it may lead to higher mortality rates. (American Geriatrics Society)

6. Avoid EEGs (electroencephalography) on Patients With Recurrent Headaches:
Recurrent headaches affect up to 20% of people. However, EEGs provide no diagnostic advantage over a clinical evaluation, do not improve treatment outcomes, and add unnecessary costs. (American Academy of Neurology)

7. Avoid Routinely Treating Acid Reflux:
Anti-reflux medications, while common for adults with GERD, are not effective for reducing symptoms in infants and may even cause harm in certain cases. (Society of Hospital Medicine)

8. Avoid Lipid Profile Tests:
The lipid profile test checks cholesterol and triglyceride levels.
Several scientific papers have proven that people with high so-called â€œbadâ€ LDL cholesterol live the longest and there is now a large number of findings that contradict the lipid hypothesis that cholesterol has to be lowered at all.Â 

9. Avoid Mammograms:
Mammograms and breast screening have had no impact on breast cancer deaths and have actually been found to increase breast cancer mortality. With toxic radiation, mammogram testing compresses sensitive breast tissue causing pain and possible tissue damage. To make matters worse, the false negative and false positive rates of mammography are a troubling 30% and 89% respectively. Another concern is that many breast cancers occur below the armpits; however, mammography completely misses this auxiliary region, viewing only the breast tissue compressed between two plates of glass. Considering these drawbacks, breast thermography should be given closer consideration. Thermography is a non-invasive and non-toxic technique which can detect abnormalities before the ons no et of a malignancy, and as early as ten years before being recognized by mammography. This makes it much safer and potentially life-saving health test for women who are unknowingly developing abnormalities, as it can take several years for a cancerous tumor to develop and be detected by a mammogram.Â 

10. Avoid PSA Testing:
A PSA blood test looks for prostate-specific antigen, a protein produced by the prostate gland. High levels are supposedly associated with prostate cancer. The problem is that the association isnâ€™t always correct, and when it is, the prostate cancer isnâ€™t necessarily deadly. Nearly 20 percent of men will be diagnosed with prostate cancer, which sounds scary, but only about 3 percent of all men die from it. The PSA test usually leads to overdiagnosis â€” biopsies and treatment in which the side effects are impotence and incontinence. Moreover, there is some evidence which suggests that biopsies and treatment actually aggravate prostate cancer. During a needle biopsy, a tumor may need to be punctured several times to retrieve an amount of tissue thatâ€™s adequate enough to be screened. It is believed that this repeated penetration may spread cancer cells into the track formed by the needle, or by spilling cancerous cells directly into the bloodstream or lympathic system.Â 

11. Avoid Routine Colorectal Cancer Screening:
Colorectal cancer screening often results in unnecessary removal of benign polyps which are of no threat to patients and the risks of their treatment or removal far exceed any benefit. The evidence is insufficient to assess the benefits and harms of computed tomographic colonography and fecal DNA testing as screening modalities for colorectal cancer.Â 

12. Avoid Dual Energy X-Ray Absorptiometry (DEXA or DXA):Â 
This technique developed in the 1980s that measures, among many things, bone mineral density. The scans can determine bone strength and signs of osteopenia, a possible precursor to osteoporosis. Limitations abound, though. Measurements vary from scan to scan of the same person, as well as from machine to machine. DEXA doesnâ€™t capture the collagen-to-mineral ratio, which is more predictive of bone strength than just mineral density. And higher bone mineral density doesnâ€™t necessarily mean stronger bones, for someone with more bone mass will have more minerals but could have weaker bones.

The ABIM initiative to reduce unnecessary testing is commendable, though it has received limited media coverage. Ideally, information on these recommendations would be displayed in clinic waiting rooms for patients to see.

I encourage everyone to understand these recommendations and make informed decisions. While these tests may still be needed in certain situations, it is wise to discuss your options with your doctor or consider a second opinion before proceeding.Â

[10/28/25, 6:39:31â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢â€¢â€¢    MY STORY   â€¢â€¢â€¢

Today, Iâ€™d like to share a personal story of how I became interested in healthy eating and lifestyle, the sources of information I rely on, and my approach to research.

Like many people, I used to believe that my diet and lifestyle were healthy and that my chronic illnesses were largely due to genetics. My dad and his brothers all had heart issues, hypertension, and diabetes. Two of them passed away from heart attacks in their 40s and 50s. I didnâ€™t smoke, and my alcohol intake was limited to a few margaritas on weekends. We were mostly vegetarian, only having meat or seafood when eating out or at gatherings. I drank a lot of milk and loved eggs, convinced they were good for me. My morning and evening tea was made mostly of milk, which I took pride in. In the summer, I enjoyed cold coffee, mango milkshakes, and Rooh Afza lassi. Most mornings, I had either fried eggs or avocado masala omelettes with parathas.

After graduating from IITK in 1971, my BMI was 22.7. I was a weightlifter and bodybuilder, even winning third-place prizes in these categories. However, over the years, my BMI gradually increased to 30.4 by the age 62. At 47, I started medication for gout. At 50, I began BP medication, and by 51, I was on diabetes medication, starting with Metformin. Within three years
Avandia, another diabetes medication, was added which was later replaced with Actos due to cardiac risks. Later, Actos was banned for similar reasons. At 54, I started thyroid medication, and by 58, I was diagnosed with sleep apnea and advised to use a CPAP machine due to loud snoring. My snoring was so loud that nobody could share a room with me at night and my wife had to use ear plugs.Â 

Our meals were largely vegetarian but high in ghee. Growing up, our meals were divided into â€œKachcha Khanaâ€ and â€œPakka Khana.â€ Lunch typically consisted of daal, roti, subji, curd, and rice, while dinner included parathis, curried potatoes, other vegetables, and curd. In the evenings, we always had snacks with tea.

In March 2012, Swami Ishwarananda of Chinmaya Mission West, with whom I was closely involved, stayed overnight at our home and requested steel cut oatmeal for breakfast. We happily prepared it, and that became the first conscious change I made to my diet. To my surprise, I began losing 1.5 pounds a month without trying, likely due to other subtle changes Iâ€™d started making unconsciously. Thatâ€™s the beauty of intentional changeâ€”it often leads to other positive habits.

On January 1, 2013, we decided to put to use the Christmas gifts from our daughters: an Apple TV and a Netflix subscription. The first documentary we watched wasÂ â€œHappyâ€, and soon after, we foundÂ â€œForks Over Knivesâ€. Since we were food lovers, we decided to watch it, and that sparked my interest in health documentaries. We watchedÂ â€œFat, Sick, and Nearly Deadâ€ and immediately ordered an Angel juicer. We joined a farm cooperative that delivered fresh organic produce twice a week, went on a three-day juice fast, adopted a vegan diet, and cut down on oil and sugar. My wife, who loves cooking, started experimenting with oil-free recipes.Â 

South Indian dishes like idli and uttapam, as well as Gujarati snacks like dhokla, handwa, and muthia, became household favorites. Tadka in our meals was replaced with chaunka (reducing ghee from 45 grams to 9 grams). Oil-based salad dressings were swapped for balsamic vinegar or lemon-ginger-honey-mustard, and we developed new soup recipes. We also adopted a practice of drinking green juice before each meal.

At the time, I wasnâ€™t aware of the benefits of intermittent or prolonged fastingâ€”that came after the 2016 Nobel Prize in Physiology. Our weekend parties still included oil and sugar, but I started noticing immediate, visible weight loss, which others commented on. My doctors, however, were cautious and encouraged me to stay on my medications. Over the next few years, I gradually phased out my medications: BP medication within 6 months, gout medication in one year, and diabetes medication took two years because of consistently high fasting glucose levels. Eventually, I also stopped my thyroid medication. My confidencte grew as friends and family noticed my changes, and soon, many of them bought green juicers, too.

In September 2017, I formed my first WhatsApp group with my IITK â€™71 batchmates. Milk and curd were the most controversial topics but, over time, some members also started following and noticed improvements. New groups formed, expanding our collective knowledge and experience.

To stay motivated, I started watching health documentaries and YouTube videos daily, learning from doctors and medical researchers. In 2015, I began attending Advanced Study weekend seminars organized by Dr. John McDougall in Santa Rosa, where I met experts like Dr. T. Colin Campbell, Dr. Caldwell Esselstyn, Dr. Michael Greger and others all of whom were very helpful in answering questions. In 2016 I began experimenting with intermittent fasting, followed by prolonged fasting in 2019.

Besides seminars, documentaries, and YouTube lectures, my primary sources of information are PubMed, books, and Google. PubMed has been invaluable for access to research on various health topics. Being retired, I had the time to dedicate 20-30 hours a week to this research, with a strong motivation to prove my doctors wrongâ€”and I did. Now, I spend even more time on the subject.

Different doctors in the plant-based, whole-foods (PBWF) movement have varying opinions. I had to choose among them and prioritize on my own. I drew heavily from my Indian cultural heritage. These decisions are mine alone and are reinforced by personal experiences and feedback from others who follow me.Â 

For example, Dr. Jason Fung advocates a keto diet with intermittent fasting, while PBWF doctors strongly oppose keto. Dr. Greger and Dr. Campbell support eating nuts, while Dr. Esselstyn is against it, especially for patients with critical heart conditions. Dr. McDougall focuses on starch, while Dr. Joel Fuhrman emphasizes fruits and vegetables. True North Health Center doctors, like Dr. Goldhammer and Dr. Klaper, focus on fasting therapy, though they have not yet introduced dry fasting as recommended by Nobel laureates Dr. Yoshinori Ohsumi (2016) and Dr. Tasuku Honjo (2018).

Lack of Fiber Question

The lack of fiber in refined sugar causes it to be absorbed rapidly in the intestines, overwhelming the liver. The liver converts the fructose portion into fat, leading to fatty liver (NAFLD), visceral fat, and insulin resistance by interfering with insulin signaling in muscle cells.

To explain this concept visually, I often show a short clip from Lucy in the Chocolate Factory (watch here).

https://youtu.be/AnHiAWlrYQc?si=sMCMxEmYv-AvU-un

In the video, when chocolates arrive at a comfortable pace, Lucy wraps them neatly and sends them down the conveyor belt to the shipping department â€” everything works smoothly. But when the belt speeds up, chaos ensues: the chocolates end up in the wrong places â€” her mouth, her hat, and even her blouse!

Our liver works in much the same way. When sugar enters the bloodstream slowly (as it does when we eat whole fruits, which contain fiber), the liver can manage it efficiently. But when sugar arrives too quickly â€” as in fruit juice or processed sweets â€” the liver becomes overwhelmed. Instead of packaging fructose into fat for safe storage in subcutaneous fat cells (under the skin), it diverts it to unhealthy destinations: liver fat, visceral fat, and intramyocellular fat (inside muscle cells). These misplaced fats cause a cascade of problems, including NAFLD, insulin resistance, and type 2 diabetes.

All this can happen simply because someone chose apple juice instead of an apple.

The Question

This raises an intriguing question:

If refined sugar (devoid of fiber) can cause such damage, what about refined fats (like oils) and refined proteins (like meat, dairy, and eggs) â€” which are also devoid of fiber?

The Answer

Letâ€™s unpack this carefully.
When we examine refined fats and refined proteins, the mechanisms differ from refined sugar â€” but the metabolic consequences are surprisingly similar. When these nutrients are consumed without the natural fiber, phytonutrients, and water found in whole plant foods, they too overload our metabolic systems.

1. Refined Fats (Oils, Ghee, Butter, etc.)

Refined fats are essentially pure triglycerides extracted from whole foods like nuts, seeds, or olives â€” stripped of fiber, antioxidants, and other protective compounds.

Key consequences:
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â Rapid absorption and extreme calorie density:
Oils are absorbed almost instantly and are the most calorie-dense foods on Earth (9 kcal/g). Even small amounts can create a large caloric surplus, promoting weight gain and visceral fat accumulation.
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â Fat accumulation in liver and muscle:
Excess dietary fat â€” especially saturated and omega-6â€“rich oils â€” infiltrates liver (hepatocytes) and muscle cells (myocytes), leading to hepatic steatosis (fatty liver) and lipotoxicity.
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â Insulin resistance:
Intracellular fat disrupts insulin signaling, reducing glucose uptake and increasing blood sugar and insulin levels â€” the same end result seen with sugar overload.
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â Inflammation:
Many refined oils (corn, sunflower, safflower, etc.) are rich in omega-6 fatty acids, which promote inflammatory compounds that worsen insulin resistance and damage blood vessels.

So yes â€” refined fats can indeed lead to fatty liver, visceral obesity, and insulin resistance, just through fat overload instead of fructose overload.

2. Refined Proteins (Meat, Eggs, Dairy)

Though not â€œrefinedâ€ in the industrial sense, animal-based foods are naturally devoid of fiber and phytonutrients, and often high in saturated fat and amino acids (like methionine and leucine) that alter metabolic pathways.

Key consequences:
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â Lack of fiber:
Without fiber, thereâ€™s no buffering of nutrient absorption, no support for gut microbes, and no binding of bile acids. This increases the metabolic burden on the liver and kidneys and promotes gut dysbiosis.
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â Fatty liver and insulin resistance:
Animal proteins often come packaged with saturated fat, contributing directly to hepatic fat accumulation. High intake of certain amino acids (especially branched-chain amino acids) activates the mTOR pathway, which interferes with insulin signaling and promotes insulin resistance.
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â Increased visceral fat:
Chronic insulin resistance caused by animal-based diets drives hyperinsulinemia, which promotes fat storage, particularly around abdominal organs.

Epidemiological studies consistently link high consumption of red and processed meats with NAFLD, type 2 diabetes, and metabolic syndrome.

3. The Unifying Concept: Fiber and Nutrient Synergy

Fiber is not just a bulking agent â€” itâ€™s a metabolic protector.

It:
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â Slows absorption of glucose, fats, and amino acids, preventing overload.
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â Feeds gut bacteria, which produce short-chain fatty acids (like butyrate) that improve insulin sensitivity and protect the liver.
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â Binds bile acids and fats, reducing lipid absorption and cholesterol levels.

When fiber is missing â€” whether from refined sugar, refined fat, or animal protein â€” metabolic overload follows. The result is lipid accumulation, oxidative stress, inflammation, and ultimately insulin resistance.

In essence, fiber and phytonutrient synergy is natureâ€™s way of ensuring that the body receives nutrients at a pace it can handle â€” just like Lucy wrapping chocolates at a steady, manageable speed. Remove the fiber, and metabolic chaos begins.

Additional Perspectives

When we try to understand something that isnâ€™t yet clear to us, it helps to view it from multiple perspectives. My post yesterday on â€œWhy Whole Foodâ€ focused on how the presence of fiber slows the absorption of food in the small intestine, preventing many lifestyle diseases. Today, letâ€™s explore a few other perspectives.

(1) Time Available for Food Absorption

Another important factor is the amount of time food remains in the small intestine for absorption.
Whole, fiber-rich foods move through the intestines more quickly, allowing less time for absorption. This has two important consequences:
* Reduced calorie absorption: Fewer calories are absorbed into the body, helping maintain a healthy weight.
* Enhanced gut health: More undigested food reaches the colon, providing nourishment for beneficial gut bacteria. These microbes flourish and, in turn, produce essential nutrients such as vitamins and short-chain fatty acids that strengthen immunity and overall health.

A good example of this is resistant starch found in beans. Because it resists absorption in the small intestine, it leads to a lower glycemic spike during the meal itself â€” less glucose enters the bloodstream since less starch is absorbed. In addition, when this resistant starch reaches the colon, it feeds beneficial bacteria that further improve metabolic health, a phenomenon known as the second meal effect.

Leaky Gut Syndrome

When food is refined and stripped of fiber, it moves slowly through the intestines, allowing nearly all calories to be absorbed before reaching the colon. This leaves the gut bacteria starved. In response, they begin consuming the mucin layer that protects the intestinal lining, eventually damaging the epithelial barrier â€” a condition known as leaky gut.

Leaky gut allows harmful substances to enter the bloodstream and is believed to be a root cause of many autoimmune diseases. Over 80 such conditions have been identified, including hypothyroidism, arthritis, and lupus â€” and the number continues to rise.

(2) The Protective Skin of Plants

The outer skin of plant foods not only protects the plant itself but is also rich in phytochemicals and antioxidants that nourish our gut microbiome. When we consume refined foods that lack these protective layers, our gut microbes are deprived of vital nutrients â€” and consequently, our bodies are deprived of the many beneficial compounds those microbes produce.

It is estimated that our microbiome may generate up to half of the chemicals required for human health â€” a staggering reminder of how deeply we depend on these microbial partners.

A Note of Humility

Many senior nutrition scientists agree that our understanding of nutrition is still in its infancy â€” there is far more we donâ€™t know than what we do. Recognizing this, we should act with humility and choose what nature has already perfected over millions of years: whole foods.

Forming the habit of eating whole, unprocessed foods is not difficult, especially when begun early in life. The habits children form when young often last a lifetime â€” and choosing whole foods is one of the most powerful habits we can pass on to the next generation.

[10/29/25, 6:56:06â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢Healthy vs Unhealthy â€¢

A common question that keeps coming up is: â€œIs this food healthy?â€
We also frequently encounter media articles comparing one food with another, making claims such as:
	1.	Jaggery is healthy
	2.	Red Wine is healthy
	3.	Olive Oil is healthy

These comparisons often confuse people because they blur the crucial difference between something being healthy and something being healthier.

Letâ€™s get this straight:
Just because one food is less harmful than another doesnâ€™t automatically make it healthy. Being â€œhealthierâ€ is a relative conceptâ€”it only means itâ€™s the lesser evil when compared to something worse. That doesnâ€™t qualify it as beneficial or safe for regular consumption. Letâ€™s look at the three examples mentioned above:

1. Jaggery vs Refined Sugar

Jaggery is often marketed as a traditional, â€œnaturalâ€ sweetener with supposed health benefits. Articles tout it as rich in minerals like iron and magnesium. But hereâ€™s the catchâ€”theyâ€™re always comparing it to refined white sugar.

Sure, if your only two options are jaggery and refined sugar, jaggery is the slightly better choice. But that does not mean it is healthy. Itâ€™s still a concentrated form of sugar and will still spike your blood glucose, burden your liver, and contribute to insulin resistance.

If youâ€™ve already managed to eliminate added sugars from your diet and are getting all the sugar you need from fruits and vegetables, please donâ€™t go back and â€œrewardâ€ yourself by adding jaggery. That would be a step backward, not forward.

Want something truly better? Eat sugarcane. It has fiber, water, and micronutrients. Much less damaging than jaggeryâ€”which is, in essence, boiled-down, dehydrated sugarcane juice minus all the good stuff.

2. Red Wine vs Other Alcohols

Red wine often gets glowing coverage in lifestyle magazines and health columns. The reason? It contains resveratrol, a plant compound with antioxidant properties that has shown some promise in lab studies.

But letâ€™s be clear: that doesnâ€™t make red wine a health food. It is still alcohol, a known toxin that damages the liver, increases cancer risk, and impairs brain function over time.

So why the good press? Itâ€™s all about context. The studies compare red wine to other alcoholic beverages. So, in that narrow context, red wine emerges as the least harmful. If you are absolutely forced to choose between red wine, white wine, and scotch (say, youâ€™re kidnapped and only these three are on offerâ€”no water, no tea, no coconut water), go with red wine. At least it brings along a few antioxidants for the ride.

But if you donâ€™t already drink alcohol, please donâ€™t startâ€”not for the resveratrol, not for â€œheart health,â€ not for social status. Even the World Health Organization is crystal clear on this: no level of alcohol consumption is completely safe.

Want resveratrol? Eat a handful of red grapes. Or better still, eat a bowl of berries, which are even richer in health-protective phytonutrientsâ€”without the alcohol, of course.

3. Olive Oil vs Other Oils

Many health-conscious Indians have now adopted extra virgin olive oil, believing itâ€™s a â€œhealthy fat.â€ The marketing works beautifully: words like â€œcold-pressed,â€ â€œextra virgin,â€ and even â€œMediterranean dietâ€ all paint olive oil as a miracle elixir.

But hereâ€™s the inconvenient truth: Olive oil is not healthy.
Itâ€™s just less harmful than things like butter, lard, or hydrogenated oils. No matter how many virgins are attached to its label, at the end of the day, itâ€™s still a refined, concentrated fatâ€”99.9% oil, stripped of all the fiber and phytonutrients found in whole olives.

Worse still, itâ€™s unsuited to Indian cooking.
We typically cook at high temperaturesâ€”think of our tadkas and deep fryingâ€”and olive oil breaks down and oxidizes quickly at those temperatures, especially extra virgin olive oil. The fumes and byproducts can be harmful.

A better alternative for high-heat Indian cooking is mustard oil, which has a high smoke point and has traditionally been used in Indian kitchens. But again, only use it if you must. Eating whole mustard seeds in your food is far healthier than consuming mustard oil.

Better yet, explore oil-free cooking methods: steaming, dry roasting, sautÃ©ing in water or vegetable broth, and baking. Youâ€™ll retain the flavors, improve your health, and save money.

In Summary:

When someone says â€œthis food is healthy,â€ ask: Healthy compared to what?

Donâ€™t fall for the trap of relative health claims.
	â€¢	Jaggery is healthier than sugarâ€”but itâ€™s not healthy.
	â€¢	Red wine is better than whiskyâ€”but itâ€™s not good for you.
	â€¢	Olive oil is less harmful than butterâ€”but itâ€™s still a processed, empty-calorie fat.

The only foods that truly promote health are whole, unrefined, plant-based foodsâ€”fruits, vegetables, legumes, whole grains, nuts, seeds, herbs, and spices.

And hereâ€™s the real rule of thumb:
If it grew on a plant, eat it.
If it was made in a plant, think twice.

[10/29/25, 6:56:06â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢Doctorsâ€™ Advice on Medications â€¢

If you are taking medication for a lifestyle disease, ask yourself: Why am I expected to take this for the rest of my life? Doctors often prescribe medications quickly because thatâ€™s what they are taught in medical school. Once youâ€™re on lifelong medication, you become a lifelong patientâ€”needing refills, follow-ups, and continued medical supervision.

In India, many patients expect a prescription; if they leave a doctorâ€™s clinic without one, they feel shortchanged. Deprescribing is rare. It isnâ€™t in the interest of doctors or pharmaceutical companies to stop medications.

Here are some observations:

1. Symptom-Focused Approach
Most doctors focus on reducing symptoms to â€œnormalâ€ ranges. The underlying causes often remain unaddressed or are oversimplifiedâ€”usually blamed on overeating or lack of exercise.

2. Prescription-Driven Practice
Doctors prescribe medication because thatâ€™s what is expected of them professionally and legally. Itâ€™s part of the standard treatment protocols they are trained to follow.

3. Lack of Nutrition Education
Doctors rarely discuss diet or lifestyle because they receive little to no training in nutrition. With limited time per patient, medication is often the quickest solution.

4. Medical Standards of Care
Doctors must follow standard care protocols to protect their licenses. In countries like the U.S., deviations can lead to malpractice lawsuits. Different specializations have different governing bodies. For example, both endocrinologists and general practitioners treat diabetes, but they may follow conflicting standards.

5. Governing Body Interests
Medical organizations often act in the interest of their members. Guidelines that lower diagnostic thresholdsâ€”meaning more patients and more prescriptionsâ€”are adopted quickly. But when new evidence supports relaxing those thresholds, changes are slow.

A case in point:
In 1996, the fasting glucose cutoff for diagnosing diabetes was lowered from 140 to 126 mg/dLâ€”quickly embraced by medical bodies. But in 2018, when guidelines were relaxed to an HbA1C of up to 8.0, it wasnâ€™t widely adopted. The American Diabetes Association (ADA) recommends 7.0, while the American Association of Clinical Endocrinologists (AACE) recommends 6.5â€”leading to confusion.

This hesitation to reverse course is human nature. Once income increases, few willingly go back. A Hindi couplet from 400 years ago says:

6. The Chronic Condition Mindset
Even when symptoms improve, doctors often discourage stopping medications. They believe the underlying disease is chronic and incurable, and improvements are solely due to ongoing medication.

Taking Charge of Your Health

As we explore various diseases in future blog posts, you will face these realities. Many people in my health group ask how to reduce or stop medications. It is inappropriate for me to provide individual medical advice. Thatâ€™s a decision between you and your doctor.

However, I aim to empower you with knowledge so you can have a more informed conversation with your physician. Adopting a plant-based whole food (PBWF) diet, intermittent fasting, and a physically active lifestyle can lead to measurable improvementsâ€”and possibly reduced reliance on medication.

When & How to Reduce Medication

Once you consistently follow a PBWF diet, walk daily, and eat early dinners, youâ€™ll likely notice better sleep and increased energy. Track your blood pressure, blood sugar, and other markers at home. If they fall below clinical thresholds, discuss reducing your medications with your doctor.

Be aware: your doctor may not be familiar with the most recent guidelines (e.g., JNC-8 for blood pressure, ACP 2018 for diabetes). You may need to bring them up and explain your lifestyle changes. If your doctor is not open to adjusting medications, consider a second opinion.

Important: I am not a doctor and do not offer medical advice. Any change in medication must be done with your physicianâ€™s guidance.

A Case Example: Dr. McDougallâ€™s Approach
Dr. John McDougall, in his 10-day live-in program in Santa Rosa, California, stopped all medications on day oneâ€”for all participants. His reasoning: when patients switched to a PBWF diet, their markers improved rapidly, and continuing medication could cause harmful drops, especially in blood sugar for diabetic patients.

This illustrates why diabetic patients must reduce insulin and medications in coordination with their doctors when switching to a healthier lifestyle.

Guiding Principles
- Never stop medications without first changing your diet and lifestyle.
- Always prioritize sustainable lifestyle improvements over quick fixes.
- Regularly monitor your progress and keep your doctor informed.
- Respect that some doctors may not believe in stopping lifelong medicationsâ€”seek a second opinion if needed.
- Understand that reducing medications safely depends on the severity of your condition and your consistency in following the new lifestyle.

Some people reduce medications by half every month, while others do so more gradually. If new guidelines suggest you were never a candidate for medication in the first place, your doctor may agree to stop it immediately.

For instance, current recommendations are:
- Blood Pressure: 140/90 for people under 60; 150/90 for those over 60
- Diabetes: HbA1C up to 8.0 is now considered acceptable in many cases

Final Note
Recent research shows that aggressively managing numbers (like glucose or BP) with medication can sometimes do more harm than good. This topic is discussed in my blog post, â€œBetter Safe Than Sorryâ€, where I debunk common medical myths.

The key takeaway: Start with food, not pharmaceuticals. As your health improves, let the numbers guide the conversationâ€”with your doctor. You deserve to know all your options.

[10/30/25, 7:13:18â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢VITAMIN D SUPPLEMENTATION: A Deeper Look

I repeatedly try to emphasize a crucial point: our understanding of nutrition is still in its infancy. Weâ€™ve only scratched the surface. When it comes to how food and nutrients interact with the body, there is far more we donâ€™t know than we do. Thatâ€™s why humility is essential when discussing nutritionâ€”especially with something as debated as vitamin D.

Dr. Robert Lustig of the University of California, San Francisco, shares a story from his first day at Cornell Medical School. During a welcome address, the Dean told incoming students: â€œHalf of what weâ€™re going to teach you over the next four years is wrong. The problem isâ€”we donâ€™t know which half.â€

The ongoing debate over vitamin D in the medical community is a perfect example of this uncertainty, as youâ€™ll see in this blog.

The Vitamin D Debate

One of the most common and hotly debated questions Iâ€™m asked is:
Should we take vitamin D supplements?

Many people today are diagnosed as â€œdeficientâ€ in vitamin D. For years, the recommended healthy range has been 30â€“50 nanograms per milliliter (ng/ml). However, many expertsâ€”including my teacher, Dr. John McDougallâ€”had questioned this range, suggesting that 20â€“40 ng/ml may be more than sufficient.

But this raises a deeper question that often goes unaddressed:
Are people who are healthy because they have high vitamin D levels?
Or do they have high vitamin D levels because theyâ€™re healthy and spend more time outdoors?

In other words: is vitamin D causally linked to good health, or is it merely associated with it?

What COVID-19 Taught Us

During the COVID-19 pandemic, several studies found that people who experienced severe symptoms often had low vitamin D levels, while those with higher levels tended to do better. This seemed to support the idea that vitamin D was protectiveâ€”but again, the same question arose:
Were their outcomes better because of vitamin D?
Or were those people already healthier due to more sun exposure and better lifestyle habits, with high vitamin D levels being just one reflection of that?

The Emerging Science: Beyond Vitamin D

Recent research offers fascinating insights into the benefits of sunlightâ€”many of which go well beyond vitamin D production.
	1.	Infrared Light and Melatonin Production
Sunlight contains a full spectrum of wavelengths from ultraviolet (UV) to infrared (IR). When our skin is exposed to IR light, it stimulates melatonin production within every cell of the bodyâ€”not just in the brain.
This melatonin acts as a powerful antioxidant, helping to reduce oxidative stress and protect mitochondrial function.
	2.	Ultraviolet Light and Vitamin D
Vitamin D is only synthesized when the skin is exposed to ultraviolet B (UVB) rays, which are shorter in wavelength. These rays are strongest around midday. However, during early mornings and late evenings, UVB rays are weaker due to the angle of the sun, while IR rays remain relatively strong.
	3.	Sunlight and Blood Sugar Control
One study on patients with diabetes showed that post-meal blood sugar spikes were significantly lower when individuals were exposed to sunlight. This again points to broader benefits of sun exposure unrelated to vitamin D.
	4.	Swedish Study on Sun Exposure and Mortality
In a remarkable 20-year study of 30,000 Swedish women, participants were grouped based on sun exposure: minimal, moderate, and high.
The findings were striking:
- Women with the highest sun exposure had the lowest mortality rates from cancer, cardiovascular, and non-cardiovascular diseases.
- Smokers in the high-sun group had the same mortality as non-smokers in the low-sun group.
In other words, lack of sun exposure was as dangerous as smoking.
	5.	U.S. COVID Study and Geography
Data also showed that people living in southern U.S. statesâ€”where sun exposure is higher year-roundâ€”had lower COVID-19 mortality rates than those in northern states.

Rethinking â€œDeficiencyâ€

These recent research highlight an important nuance:
Time of day matters.

If someone gets regular morning or evening sun, they may benefit greatly from infrared exposure, which supports cellular healthâ€”but their vitamin D levels may still be low, simply because UVB rays are weak at those times.

This means a person could have low vitamin D levels on paper yet still be in excellent health. On the flip side, someone supplementing to maintain high vitamin D levels might assume they are healthy, when in fact they are missing out on the broader benefits of real sunlight.

To Conclude:

Sunlight is more important than vitamin D. Even 15â€“20 minutes of sun exposure at any time of day supports mitochondrial function and reduces the risk of major metabolic diseasesâ€”including diabetes, cardiovascular disease, and even cancer.

For optimal vitamin D production, mid-morning (about 2 hours after sunrise) to mid-afternoon (2 hours before sunset) is the best time to get direct sun.

But donâ€™t fall into the trap of thinking that a normal vitamin D levelâ€”achieved through supplementsâ€”means youâ€™re fine. Itâ€™s not just about the number on the lab report. Itâ€™s about the quality of your sun exposure and its far-reaching effects on your overall health.

[10/30/25, 7:13:19â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢Food Science, Nutrition, and Metabolic Healthâ€¢

In the 1920s, research on celiac disease experienced by sailors on long voyages led to the discovery of vitamin C. At the time, the conclusion was not that the real issue was a lack of fresh fruits and vegetables, but rather a deficiency of a specific nutrient. This marked the beginning of what we now consider nutrition science.

Similarly, the development of antibiotics also in 1928 gave rise to modern health science, which often embraces the idea of â€œa pill for every ill.â€ These approaches served us well for decades. However, a growing number of doctors and nutritionists are beginning to recognize that we may have made some critical errors in our understanding.

Dr. Colin Campbell, the number one nutritionist in the world, for example, has spoken out against the reductionist view of nutritionâ€”where the focus is placed on individual nutrients rather than the complex interactions among them in whole plant foods. Just because a nutrient is isolated and supplemented doesnâ€™t mean the body will absorb and utilize it effectively.

The emerging field of epigenetics has further challenged traditional views. It has shown us that bacteria are not necessarily our enemiesâ€”in fact, they are essential partners, producing many of the chemicals our bodies need. Every plant food has its own unique bacterial profile, which helps colonize our gut. Our immunity and overall health are deeply tied to the well-being of this internal ecosystem. Antibiotics, while sometimes necessary, can harm this microbial balance and have long-term consequences.

We seem to have come full circle, realizing that some of what we once thought was harmful may not be, and vice versa. Paradigms are shifting, and many of the lessons from the past century must be reconsidered.

New Insights
New insights continue to reshape our understanding. For example, the Nobel Prizes awarded in 2016, 2017, and 2018 highlighted groundbreaking discoveries in areas such as autophagy and circadian biology. These findings show that the absence of foodâ€”and the timing of food intakeâ€”can be just as important as the food itself. This has profound implications for how we approach diet and lifestyle choices today.

Food Science, Nutrition & Metabolic Health
Food science, nutrition, and metabolic health are three distinct fields that are often misunderstood or used interchangeably. Hereâ€™s what each actually means:
* Food Science deals with what happens to food from the time itâ€™s grown until it reaches your mouthâ€”processing, storage, preservation, and preparation.
* Nutrition concerns what happens to food after it enters your mouthâ€”how itâ€™s digested, absorbed, and transported to your cells.
* Metabolic Health focuses on what happens to food inside your cellsâ€”how itâ€™s used to produce energy and support cellular function.

The Real Problem: Metabolic Dysfunction

Many chronic diseasesâ€”such as type 2 diabetes, hypertension, cardiovascular disease, dyslipidemia, fatty liver, cancer, dementia, and PCOSâ€”stem from metabolic dysfunction at the cellular level. These conditions account for nearly 75% of the entire U.S. healthcare budget. Despite this, our current medical approach largely focuses on symptom management rather than root-cause resolution. Here are some examples. 

	1.	Heart Disease:
We use statins to lower LDL cholesterol, but LDL is not the root problemâ€”itâ€™s a symptom or marker of an underlying issue. Thatâ€™s why using statins for primary prevention doesnâ€™t significantly extend lifespan: weâ€™re not fixing the real problem.
	2.	Hypertension:
We treat high blood pressure with medications, but weâ€™re not addressing endothelial dysfunctionâ€”the underlying issue that leads to kidney failure, heart failure, and more. Lowering blood pressure helps, but itâ€™s a temporary patch, not a cure.
	3.	High Blood Glucose:
We prescribe drugs to reduce blood sugar, but weâ€™re not correcting insulin resistance or pancreatic beta-cell dysfunction, which are the actual causes. Again, lowering blood glucose isnâ€™t bad, but it doesnâ€™t fix the metabolic defects that led to it in the first place. Weâ€™re managing symptoms while ignoring the disease mechanism.

For the past seven decades, this symptom-based approach hasnâ€™t solved the problem. Itâ€™s time to shift our mindset and focus on the root causeâ€”mitochondrial dysfunction.

The Role of Mitochondria
Mitochondria are the powerhouses of the cell, generating ATPâ€”the chemical energy our bodies depend on. Without ATP, cells cannot function. When mitochondria fail, cells must shift to a less efficient energy system: fermentation instead of oxidation.

For example, cancer cells can survive without oxygen by relying heavily on glucose. Why? Because their mitochondria are damaged or entirely nonfunctional. This mitochondrial dysfunction is at the core of many metabolic diseases.

Rethinking Our Belief Systems
Much like religion, nutrition has long been treated as a matter of dogma. People change their religion usually because their current beliefs no longer serve them. The same is happening in nutrition.

People are questioning outdated ideas. For decades, weâ€™ve used a reductionist approach to food, focusing on isolated nutrients and calorie counting. But this has failed to improve public health. Experts like Dr. T. Colin Campbell and Dr. Robert Lustig have worked to challenge and dismantle these flawed beliefs. Dr. Campbell teaches that we should think of food as a whole, not just as a sum of its nutrient parts. Every food contains a unique â€œsymphonyâ€ of nutrients that work together in ways weâ€™re only beginning to understand.

[10/30/25, 7:13:19â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢Healthy vs Unhealthy â€¢

A common question that keeps coming up is: â€œIs this food healthy?â€
We also frequently encounter media articles comparing one food with another, making claims such as:
	1.	Jaggery is healthy
	2.	Red Wine is healthy
	3.	Olive Oil is healthy

These comparisons often confuse people because they blur the crucial difference between something being healthy and something being healthier.

Letâ€™s get this straight:
Just because one food is less harmful than another doesnâ€™t automatically make it healthy. Being â€œhealthierâ€ is a relative conceptâ€”it only means itâ€™s the lesser evil when compared to something worse. That doesnâ€™t qualify it as beneficial or safe for regular consumption. Letâ€™s look at the three examples mentioned above:

1. Jaggery vs Refined Sugar

Jaggery is often marketed as a traditional, â€œnaturalâ€ sweetener with supposed health benefits. Articles tout it as rich in minerals like iron and magnesium. But hereâ€™s the catchâ€”theyâ€™re always comparing it to refined white sugar.

Sure, if your only two options are jaggery and refined sugar, jaggery is the slightly better choice. But that does not mean it is healthy. Itâ€™s still a concentrated form of sugar and will still spike your blood glucose, burden your liver, and contribute to insulin resistance.

If youâ€™ve already managed to eliminate added sugars from your diet and are getting all the sugar you need from fruits and vegetables, please donâ€™t go back and â€œrewardâ€ yourself by adding jaggery. That would be a step backward, not forward.

Want something truly better? Eat sugarcane. It has fiber, water, and micronutrients. Much less damaging than jaggeryâ€”which is, in essence, boiled-down, dehydrated sugarcane juice minus all the good stuff.

2. Red Wine vs Other Alcohols

Red wine often gets glowing coverage in lifestyle magazines and health columns. The reason? It contains resveratrol, a plant compound with antioxidant properties that has shown some promise in lab studies.

But letâ€™s be clear: that doesnâ€™t make red wine a health food. It is still alcohol, a known toxin that damages the liver, increases cancer risk, and impairs brain function over time.

So why the good press? Itâ€™s all about context. The studies compare red wine to other alcoholic beverages. So, in that narrow context, red wine emerges as the least harmful. If you are absolutely forced to choose between red wine, white wine, and scotch (say, youâ€™re kidnapped and only these three are on offerâ€”no water, no tea, no coconut water), go with red wine. At least it brings along a few antioxidants for the ride.

But if you donâ€™t already drink alcohol, please donâ€™t startâ€”not for the resveratrol, not for â€œheart health,â€ not for social status. Even the World Health Organization is crystal clear on this: no level of alcohol consumption is completely safe.

Want resveratrol? Eat a handful of red grapes. Or better still, eat a bowl of berries, which are even richer in health-protective phytonutrientsâ€”without the alcohol, of course.

3. Olive Oil vs Other Oils

Many health-conscious Indians have now adopted extra virgin olive oil, believing itâ€™s a â€œhealthy fat.â€ The marketing works beautifully: words like â€œcold-pressed,â€ â€œextra virgin,â€ and even â€œMediterranean dietâ€ all paint olive oil as a miracle elixir.

But hereâ€™s the inconvenient truth: Olive oil is not healthy.
Itâ€™s just less harmful than things like butter, lard, or hydrogenated oils. No matter how many virgins are attached to its label, at the end of the day, itâ€™s still a refined, concentrated fatâ€”99.9% oil, stripped of all the fiber and phytonutrients found in whole olives.

Worse still, itâ€™s unsuited to Indian cooking.
We typically cook at high temperaturesâ€”think of our tadkas and deep fryingâ€”and olive oil breaks down and oxidizes quickly at those temperatures, especially extra virgin olive oil. The fumes and byproducts can be harmful.

A better alternative for high-heat Indian cooking is mustard oil, which has a high smoke point and has traditionally been used in Indian kitchens. But again, only use it if you must. Eating whole mustard seeds in your food is far healthier than consuming mustard oil.

Better yet, explore oil-free cooking methods: steaming, dry roasting, sautÃ©ing in water or vegetable broth, and baking. Youâ€™ll retain the flavors, improve your health, and save money.

In Summary:

When someone says â€œthis food is healthy,â€ ask: Healthy compared to what?

Donâ€™t fall for the trap of relative health claims.
	â€¢	Jaggery is healthier than sugarâ€”but itâ€™s not healthy.
	â€¢	Red wine is better than whiskyâ€”but itâ€™s not good for you.
	â€¢	Olive oil is less harmful than butterâ€”but itâ€™s still a processed, empty-calorie fat.

And hereâ€™s the real rule of thumb:
If it grew on a plant, eat it.
If it was made in a plant, think twice.
