[10/30/25, 7:13:20â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢â€¢ BREAKFAST OR NOT? â€¢â€¢ 

Technically speaking, breakfast is simply the first meal of the day, no matter when you eat it. Whether itâ€™s 7 a.m., noon, or 3 p.m.â€”whenever you break your overnight fast, that is your â€œbreak-fast.â€

However, in common usage, breakfast is almost always associated with the early morningâ€”a meal eaten shortly after waking up. This association is relatively modern in the sweep of human history.

A Brief History of â€œBreakfastâ€

The word breakfast appeared in written English during the 15th century, referring literally to the act of â€œbreaking the fastâ€ from the night before. In Old English, people used the word morgenmete, which simply meant â€œmorning meal.â€

Throughout much of human history, meals were shaped by necessity, not routine. People often ate one or two meals a day depending on their lifestyle, work, or availability of food. Regular morning meals were not always standard practice.

In fact, in many traditional cultures, people began their day with work or activity and did not eat immediately upon waking. Even today, in many parts of rural India or Africa, breakfast as we know it is minimal or delayed.

The Birth of the Modern â€œBreakfastâ€

The cultural and commercial rise of the modern breakfast owes much to one man: Dr. John Harvey Kellogg, a physician, health reformer, and Seventh-day Adventist who ran the Battle Creek Sanitarium in Michigan in the late 1800s.

Kellogg was obsessed with digestive health and bowel regularity. He was a strong proponent of bland, fiber-rich, meatless diets and believed that spicy or rich foods (as well as masturbation) led to poor health. He promoted enemas, hydrotherapy, and vegetarianism as tools for maintaining internal purity.

In his quest to develop a healthy, palatable food for his patients, he invented Corn Flakes, along with his brother, Will Keith Kellogg. Originally, the flakes were entirely plain and unsweetenedâ€”intended as a bland, wholesome food to calm the passions and nourish the gut.

Will Kellogg, however, saw commercial potential in this invention. He eventually added sugar to the flakes and began mass marketing them as a ready-to-eat breakfast cereal. The Kellogg Company was born, and with it began the commercialization of breakfast on a massive scale.

To help promote cereal, the idea that â€œbreakfast is the most important meal of the dayâ€ was popularized. The phrase first appeared in Good Health magazine in 1917, written by Lenna F. Cooper, a nurse and dietitian trained under Dr. Kellogg. This slogan became a marketing hookâ€”one that stuck, thanks to decades of advertising.

The Processed Food Industry Takes Over

Today, breakfast cereals are a multi-billion dollar industry. Aisles in grocery stores are lined with boxes claiming health benefits, energy boosts, or vitamin enrichment. Many of these cereals are loaded with sugar, preservatives, artificial flavors, and refined grainsâ€”a far cry from Kelloggâ€™s original idea of health.

Despite these nutritional drawbacks, the belief that breakfast is essentialâ€”especially cereal-based breakfastâ€”remains deeply embedded in modern culture. But science is beginning to challenge this long-standing assumption.

Rethinking Breakfast in Light of Modern Science

Recent studies suggest that breakfast is not necessarily the most important meal of the dayâ€”especially not for everyone.

In fact, for individuals suffering from chronic metabolic conditionsâ€”such as:
	â€¢	Type 2 diabetes
	â€¢	Hypertension
	â€¢	Obesity
	â€¢	Fatty liver
	â€¢	Metabolic syndrome

â€”skipping or delaying breakfast may actually be beneficial.

Why? Because the human body is not designed to be in a constant fed state. When we eat frequently and start early, we donâ€™t allow the body time to repair, recycle, or switch metabolic gears.

Intermittent Fasting: The Metabolic Shift

The concept of intermittent fasting (IF) has gained scientific traction in recent years. One of the most common and effective patterns is the 16:8 method, where you fast for 16 hours and eat within an 8-hour window.

This method works with the bodyâ€™s natural rhythm. Hereâ€™s how:
	â€¢	The liver stores about 500 calories worth of glucose (glycogen), which is used up in roughly 10â€“12 hours after your last meal.
	â€¢	Once glycogen is depleted, the body switches to burning fat for energyâ€”entering a state called ketosis.
	â€¢	This fat-burning phase reduces visceral fat, liver fat, and intramyocellular lipids (fat stored in muscle).
	â€¢	It also triggers autophagy, a cellular clean-up process that helps prevent disease and promotes longevity. This process earned the 2016 Nobel Prize in Medicine.

So, by delaying or skipping breakfast, we allow the body to transition from glucose burning to fat burning, giving our organs a much-needed metabolic break.

But What If You Do Eat Breakfast?

If your lifestyle or health condition doesnâ€™t require fastingâ€”or if youâ€™re still easing into the practiceâ€”you can still make breakfast a nutritious and healing meal.

Hereâ€™s an ideal approach to break-fast when youâ€™re ready to eat:
	1.	Start with a Green Juice or Smoothie
Made from spinach, kale, green apples, amla, coriander, celery, cucumber, lemon, ginger etc. This provides chlorophyll, minerals, and enzymes.
	2.	Follow with a Large Serving of Fruits
Fruits are rich in water, fiber, and natural sugars. They hydrate, cleanse the system, and provide quick energy without burdening digestion.
	3.	Finish with a Whole Grain or Fermented Food
	â€¢	Oatmeal is excellentâ€”rich in soluble fiber, it helps lower LDL cholesterol and keeps you full longer.
	â€¢	Idly with chutney (not sambhar) is another great option. Idly is fermented and steamed; chutney can be made oil-free. Avoid sambhar if it contains oil, as that adds unnecessary fat and can promote inflammation.

Final Thoughts

The blanket statement that â€œbreakfast is the most important meal of the dayâ€ needs to be revisited in light of modern evidence. While breakfast might still be important for children, athletes, or those with special nutritional needs, it is not a universal ruleâ€”and certainly not a sacred one.

If youâ€™re trying to reverse chronic illness, improve your metabolism, or lose excess fat, skipping or delaying breakfast and narrowing your eating window may be more effective than focusing on what you eat in the morning.

Letâ€™s unlearn the myths handed down by food marketing, and instead make decisions based on biology, history, and personal health goals. Whether you eat breakfast or notâ€”eat consciously, not conventionally.

[10/30/25, 7:13:21â€¯PM] ~â€¯Meenakshi Nigam: â€¢Omega-3:ALA, DHA, EPAâ€¢

The human body is remarkably efficient at converting excess calories into fat. This mechanism evolved as a survival strategyâ€”storing energy during times of abundance to sustain us during times of scarcity. Fat is found in all plant-based foods, and consuming just 10â€“15% of our daily calories from fat is more than sufficient for health.

There are three main types of fats:
- Saturated Fats: Primarily found in animal products. Among plant sources, coconut and palm kernel oil are notable examples.
- Monounsaturated Fats: Found in nuts, avocados, olives, and mustard seeds.
- Polyunsaturated Fats: This group includes essential fats like omega-3 (found in dark leafy greens, walnuts, flaxseeds, chia seeds) and omega-6 (found in corn, sunflower, safflower, and cottonseed oils).

Of all these fats, only two fatty acids are considered essentialâ€”meaning the body cannot produce them on its own and they must be obtained through food. These are omega-3 and omega-6. Other fats are not considered essential.

Omega-3 fats exist in three forms:
- ALA (alpha-linolenic acid) â€“ Found in plant foods like flaxseeds, chia seeds, and walnuts.
- DHA (docosahexaenoic acid) and
- EPA (eicosapentaenoic acid) â€“ Found primarily in animal foods, especially fish.

The human body can convert ALA (from plant sources) into DHA and EPA, although this conversion rate is sometimes questioned. Based on certain studies, some experts suggest that the conversion may be inadequate, and therefore DHA/EPA supplements should be taken.

However, I do not agree with this viewâ€”for the following reasons:
- Evolutionary Logic: My core philosophy is grounded in the belief that the human body, shaped by millions of years of evolution, knows how to process natural, whole foods. If we simply eat whole, plant-based foods as intended by nature, the body can take care of itself. Supplements are often unnecessaryâ€”and potentially harmful. The only exception is Vitamin B12, which is no longer naturally available due to water chlorination and modern hygiene practices. Suggesting that the body cannot convert enough ALA into DHA and EPA contradicts this evolutionary logic.
- Differing Expert Opinions: Even among leading voices in the plant-based, whole-food (PBWF) community, there are differences in opinion. In such cases, I choose to align with those whose views resonate with my experience and understanding. I personally discussed this issue with Dr. T. Colin Campbell and Dr. John McDougall, both of whom advise against DHA/EPA supplementation. Dr. Campbell offered the following statement:

â€œIf I must answer the DHA question, I would only say that we can get plenty of that type of chemical from the consumption of the omega-3 fatty acids that are found in certain plantsâ€”certain nuts, flaxseed, etc. Indeed, it also is related to a dietary balance of omega-3 to omega-6 fatty acids, and this balance can be readily met with a good-quality diet of wholesome vegetables, fruits, grains, and nuts.â€

For further insight, I highly recommend reading the following article.

Confessions of a Fish Killer

This course has explored the five pillars of health and the forces that shape our understanding of it. The twelve-point guidelines shared here can help anyone live a healthy, disease-free life.

Even health-conscious people can make poor choices when guided by misinformation. Modern medicine often suppresses symptoms rather than addressing root causes. Yet true healing begins with correcting diet and lifestyle. Hippocrates taught that food and daily habits form the foundation of healthâ€”a principle lost when medicine shifted toward drugs after the early 1900s.

Industrialization of food for profit replaced nourishment with convenience. As fiber disappeared and physical activity declined, lifestyle diseases emerged. These cannot be cured by medication; drugs may normalize numbers but leave the cause untouched. The health advice we receive is often influenced by industries that benefit from dependency on their products.

Common myths persist:
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â High protein intake is essential.
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â Dairy is required for strong bones.
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â â€œEverything in moderationâ€ is healthy.
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â A calorie is a calorie, regardless of source.
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â Chronic diseases are incurable.

Science tells a different story. Most chronic illnesses result from poor diet and lifestyleâ€”and when these are corrected, the body begins to heal. Symptoms are not enemies but messages from within, urging us to change. Suppressing them with medication is like silencing the messenger.

The body continually adjusts to unhealthy habits, and these compensations are often mistaken for disease. Medications that aim to â€œfixâ€ biomarkers may disturb natural balance and give false comfort. Real recovery begins when we align with nature and allow the body to restore itself.

Reducing Medications

After adopting a whole-food, plant-based diet, regular walking, and early dinners, most people see improved energy, sleep, and blood markers. Track your progress at home and, when numbers improve, consult your physician about gradually tapering medication. Always change your diet before reducing drugs, and never stop medication without guidance.

Updated guidelines recommend more moderate targets:
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â Blood pressure below 140/90 for those under 60, and 150/90 for those over 60.
Â Â Â â€¢Â Â Â HbA1C up to 8.0 for diabetics.

If your doctor is unaware of these updates or hesitant to adjust prescriptions, consider a second opinion. Medication for lifestyle diseases should never be lifelong if underlying causes are removed.

Reclaiming Our Health

During the last century, materialism and profit-driven science distorted healthcare and nutrition. Medical education sidelined prevention and neglected nutrition training. Food science, focused on taste and shelf life, stripped fiber and nutrients from our meals. The result has been damaged microbiomes and epidemics of metabolic and inflammatory disease.

Reductionist science treated nutrients in isolation instead of understanding food as a whole. Medicine, built around symptom suppression, joined hands with industries that profit when people stay sick. Drugs that mask symptoms often work against the bodyâ€™s natural healing, accelerating decline.

To reclaim our health, we must move from managing disease to eliminating its causes. That means choosing whole, unprocessed plant foods, restoring fiber, staying active, sleeping early, and respecting the bodyâ€™s innate intelligence. This course was created to bring these truths to light and offer a path to sustainable well-being.

Science is an ever-evolving quest filled with what I call Not Yet Knowns (NYKnowns). Our grandchildren will know far more than we doâ€”perhaps even laugh at the idea that medicine was once used to treat illness.

Through my own recovery and guiding thousands of others, Iâ€™ve learned that the solutions are few and simple. The same diet and lifestyle that prevent one chronic disease prevent them all. The American Gut Project confirmed that eating a wide variety of plant foods builds a diverse microbiome and stronger immunity. The same likely applies to our lungs and skinâ€”diversity in diet and environment builds resilience.

Our bodies are complex, but the principles of health are simple. Even sunlightâ€”once valued only for vitamin Dâ€”is now known to generate melatonin within cells and promote deeper healing. What once seemed a â€œcauseâ€ may simply be a marker of good health.

My wish is that you carry this knowledge forwardâ€”to apply it, to share it, and to help create a world where health is our natural state.
